<p><b>Latin</b> (Latin: <span lang="la"><i>lingua latīna</i></span>, <small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Latin" title="Help:IPA for Latin">[ˈlɪŋɡʷa laˈtiːna]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Italic_script#Etruscan_alphabet" title="Old Italic script">Etruscan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabets</a>.</p>
<p>Latin was originally spoken in <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Through the power of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, it became the dominant language, initially in Italy and subsequently throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> developed into the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>. <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_words_with_English_derivatives" title="List of Latin words with English derivatives">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Italian_origin" title="List of English words of Italian origin">Italian</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_French_origin" title="List of English words of French origin">French</a> have contributed many words to the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>. Latin and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> roots are used in <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_and_Greek_words_commonly_used_in_systematic_names" title="List of Latin and Greek words commonly used in systematic names">biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_medical_roots,_suffixes_and_prefixes" title="List of medical roots, suffixes and prefixes">medicine</a>.</p>
<p>By the late Roman Republic (75 BC), <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> had been <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standardised</a> into <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> was the colloquial form spoken during the same time and attested in <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">inscriptions</a> and the works of comic playwrights like <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">Terence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a> is the written language from the 3rd century, and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> the language used from the 9th century to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Renaissance" title="The Renaissance">Renaissance</a> which used <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Latin" title="Renaissance Latin">Renaissance Latin</a>. Later, Early Modern Latin and <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">Modern Latin</a> evolved. Latin was used as the language of international communication, scholarship, and science until well into the 18th century, when it began to be supplanted by <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernaculars</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Latin" title="Ecclesiastical Latin">Ecclesiastical Latin</a> remains the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.</p>
<p>Today, many students, scholars and members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_orders_(Catholic_Church)" title="Holy orders (Catholic Church)">Catholic clergy</a> speak Latin fluently. It is taught in primary, secondary and postsecondary educational institutions around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Latin is a highly <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">inflected language</a>, with three distinct <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">genders</a>, seven <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">noun cases</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">verb conjugations</a>, four verb principal parts, six <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">persons</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">moods</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Voice_(grammar)" title="Voice (grammar)">voices</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspects</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">numbers</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The language has been passed down through various forms.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inscriptions">Inscriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Inscriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some inscriptions have been published in an internationally agreed, monumental, multivolume series, the "<i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Inscriptionum_Latinarum" title="Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum">Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum</a></i> (CIL)". Authors and publishers vary, but the format is about the same: volumes detailing inscriptions with a critical apparatus stating the <a href="/wiki/Provenance" title="Provenance">provenance</a> and relevant information. The reading and interpretation of these inscriptions is the subject matter of the field of <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a>. About 270,000 inscriptions are known.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="640" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/300px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/450px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/600px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i> is one of the most famous classical Latin texts of the Golden Age of Latin. The unvarnished, journalistic style of this <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a> general has long been taught as a model of the urbane Latin officially spoken and written in the floruit of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The works of several hundred ancient authors who wrote in Latin have survived in whole or in part, in substantial works or in fragments to be analyzed in <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>. They are in part the subject matter of the field of <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a>. Their works were published in <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscript</a> form before the invention of printing and are now published in carefully annotated printed editions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Loeb_Classical_Library" title="Loeb Classical Library">Loeb Classical Library</a>, published by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Press" title="Harvard University Press">Harvard University Press</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Classical_Texts" title="Oxford Classical Texts">Oxford Classical Texts</a>, published by <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_modern_literature" title="Latin translations of modern literature">Latin translations of modern literature</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island" title="Treasure Island">Treasure Island</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe">Robinson Crusoe</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Paddington_Bear" title="Paddington Bear">Paddington Bear</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winnie_the_Pooh" title="Winnie the Pooh">Winnie the Pooh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">The Adventures of Tintin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Asterix" title="Asterix">Asterix</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter" title="Harry Potter">Harry Potter</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Walter_the_Farting_Dog" title="Walter the Farting Dog">Walter the Farting Dog</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Prince" title="The Little Prince">Le Petit Prince</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Max_and_Moritz" title="Max and Moritz">Max and Moritz</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/How_the_Grinch_Stole_Christmas!" title="How the Grinch Stole Christmas!">How the Grinch Stole Christmas!</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cat_in_the_Hat" title="The Cat in the Hat">The Cat in the Hat</a></i>, and a book of fairy tales, "fabulae mirabiles," are intended to garner popular interest in the language. Additional resources include phrasebooks and resources for rendering everyday phrases and concepts into Latin, such as <a href="/wiki/Meissner%27s_Latin_Phrasebook" title="Meissner's Latin Phrasebook">Meissner's Latin Phrasebook</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistics">Linguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Linguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Latin_influence_in_English" title="Latin influence in English">Latin influence in English</a> has been significant at all stages of its insular development. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, borrowing from Latin occurred from ecclesiastical usage established by Saint <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a> in the 6th century or indirectly after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman language</a>. From the 16th to the 18th centuries, English writers cobbled together huge numbers of new words from Latin and Greek words, dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Inkhorn_term" title="Inkhorn term">inkhorn terms</a>", as if they had spilled from a pot of ink. Many of these words were used once by the author and then forgotten, but some useful ones survived, such as 'imbibe' and 'extrapolate'. Many of the most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysyllabic" title="Polysyllabic">polysyllabic</a> English words are of Latin origin through the medium of <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a>.</p>
<p>The influence of Roman governance and <a href="/wiki/Roman_technology" title="Roman technology">Roman technology</a> on the less-developed nations under Roman dominion made those nations adopted Latin phraseology in some specialized areas, such as science, technology, medicine, and law. For example, <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">the Linnaean system</a> of plant and animal classification was heavily influenced by <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Historia Naturalis</a></i>, an encyclopedia of people, places, plants, animals, and things published by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>. Roman medicine, recorded in the works of such physicians as <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, established that today's <a href="/wiki/Medical_terminology" title="Medical terminology">medical terminology</a> would be primarily derived from Latin and Greek words, the Greek being filtered through the Latin. Roman engineering had the same effect on <a href="/wiki/Scientific_terminology" title="Scientific terminology">scientific terminology</a> as a whole. Latin law principles have survived partly in a long <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_legal_terms" title="List of Latin legal terms">list of Latin legal terms</a>.</p>
<p>A few <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international auxiliary languages</a> have been heavily influenced by Latin. <a href="/wiki/Interlingua" title="Interlingua">Interlingua</a> is sometimes considered a simplified, modern version of the language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2017)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Latin#Dubious" title="Talk:Latin">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latino_sine_Flexione" title="Latino sine Flexione">Latino sine Flexione</a>, popular in the early 20th century, is Latin with its inflections stripped away, among other grammatical changes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latin_dictionary.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1408" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/220px-Latin_dictionary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/330px-Latin_dictionary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/440px-Latin_dictionary.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latin_dictionary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A multivolume Latin dictionary in the <a href="/wiki/University_Library_of_Graz" title="University Library of Graz">University Library of Graz</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout European history, an education in the classics was considered crucial for those who wished to join literate circles. <a href="/wiki/Instruction_in_Latin" title="Instruction in Latin">Instruction in Latin</a> is an essential aspect. In today's world, a large number of Latin students in the US learn from <i>Wheelock's Latin: The Classic Introductory Latin Course, Based on Ancient Authors</i>. This book, first published in 1956,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> was written by <a href="/wiki/Frederic_M._Wheelock" title="Frederic M. Wheelock">Frederic M. Wheelock</a>, who received a PhD from Harvard University. <i>Wheelock's Latin</i> has become the standard text for many American introductory Latin courses.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_Latin" title="Living Latin">Living Latin</a> movement attempts to teach Latin in the same way that living languages are taught, as a means of both spoken and written communication. It is available at the Vatican and at some institutions in the US, such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kentucky" title="University of Kentucky">University of Kentucky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University" title="Iowa State University">Iowa State University</a>. The British <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a> is a major supplier of Latin textbooks for all levels, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Latin_Course" title="Cambridge Latin Course">Cambridge Latin Course</a> series. It has also published a subseries of children's texts in Latin by Bell &amp; Forte, which recounts the adventures of a mouse called <a href="/wiki/Minimus" title="Minimus">Minimus</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg/200px-Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg/300px-Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg/400px-Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Latin and Ancient Greek at <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>, 2014.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Association" title="Classical Association">Classical Association</a> encourages the study of antiquity through various means, such as publications and grants. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> a number of prestigious independent schools, for example <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harrow_School" title="Harrow School">Harrow</a>, and Via Facilis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> a London-based charity, run Latin courses. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Classical_League" title="American Classical League">American Classical League</a> supports every effort to further the study of classics. Its subsidiaries include the <a href="/wiki/National_Junior_Classical_League" title="National Junior Classical League">National Junior Classical League</a> (with more than 50,000 members), which encourages high school students to pursue the study of Latin, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Senior_Classical_League" title="National Senior Classical League">National Senior Classical League</a>, which encourages students to continue their study of the classics into college. The league also sponsors the <a href="/wiki/National_Latin_Exam" title="National Latin Exam">National Latin Exam</a>. Classicist <a href="/wiki/Mary_Beard_(classicist)" title="Mary Beard (classicist)">Mary Beard</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times_Literary_Supplement" title="The Times Literary Supplement">The Times Literary Supplement</a></i> in 2006 that the reason for learning Latin is because of what was written in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timesonline_train_the_brain_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline_train_the_brain-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin was or is the official language of European states:</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Flag_of_the_Vatican_City.svg/15px-Flag_of_the_Vatican_City.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Flag_of_the_Vatican_City.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_Vatican_City.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Flag_of_the_Vatican_City.svg/30px-Flag_of_the_Vatican_City.svg.png 2x" width="15"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> – used in the <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> being the official language of <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_Hungary.svg/23px-Flag_of_Hungary.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_Hungary.svg/35px-Flag_of_Hungary.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_Hungary.svg/46px-Flag_of_Hungary.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> - Latin was the sole official language of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> from the 11th century to the mid 19th century, when it was replaced by Hungarian in 1844. The best known Latin language poet originating from Hungary was <a href="/wiki/Janus_Pannonius" title="Janus Pannonius">Janus Pannonius</a>.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> – Latin was the official language of <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Parliament" title="Croatian Parliament">Croatian Parliament</a> (Sabor) from the 13th to the 19th century (1847). The oldest preserved records of the parliamentary sessions (Congregatio Regni totius Sclavonie generalis) – held in Zagreb (Zagabria), Croatia – date from 19 April 1273. An extensive <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Latin_literature" title="Croatian Latin literature">Croatian Latin literature</a> exists.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/46px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> – officially recognised and widely used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> between the 9th and 18th centuries, commonly used in foreign relations and popular as a second language among some of the nobility<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedrich_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Latin" title="History of Latin">History of Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="2232" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png/330px-Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png/495px-Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png/660px-Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The linguistic landscape of Central Italy at the beginning of Roman expansion</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of historical phases of the language have been recognised, each distinguished by subtle differences in vocabulary, usage, spelling, morphology and syntax. There are no hard and fast rules of classification; different scholars emphasise different features. As a result, the list has variants, as well as alternative names. In addition to the historical phases, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Latin" title="Ecclesiastical Latin">Ecclesiastical Latin</a> refers to the styles used by the writers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> as well as by Protestant scholars from <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> onward.</p>
<p>After the Western Roman Empire fell in 476, and Germanic kingdoms took its place, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic people</a> adopted Latin as a language more suitable for legal and other formal uses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Latin">Old Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Old Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a></div>
<p>The earliest known form of Latin is Old Latin, which was spoken from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Roman Kingdom</a> to the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> period. It is attested both in inscriptions and in some of the earliest extant Latin literary works, such as the comedies of <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">Terence</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> was devised from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscan_alphabet" title="Etruscan alphabet">Etruscan alphabet</a>. The writing later changed from an initial right-to-left or <a href="/wiki/Boustrophedon" title="Boustrophedon">boustrophedon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> to a left-to-right script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Latin">Classical Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Classical Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a></div>
<p>During the late republic and into the first years of the empire, a new Classical Latin arose, a conscious creation of the orators, poets, historians and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literate" title="Literate">literate</a> men, who wrote the great works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_literature" title="Classical literature">classical literature</a>, which were taught in <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> schools. Today's instructional grammars trace their roots to such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_school" title="Roman school">schools</a>, which served as a sort of informal language academy dedicated to maintaining and perpetuating educated speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulgar_Latin">Vulgar Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Vulgar Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a></div>
<p>Philological analysis of Archaic Latin works, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a>, which contain snippets of everyday speech, indicates that a spoken language, Vulgar Latin (termed <i>sermo vulgi</i>, "the speech of the masses", by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>), existed at the same time as the literate Classical Latin. The informal language was rarely written, so philologists have been left with only individual words and phrases cited by classical authors and those found as graffiti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>As it was free to develop on its own, there is no reason to suppose that the speech was uniform either diachronically or geographically. On the contrary, romanised European populations developed their own dialects of the language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">Decline of the Roman Empire</a> meant a deterioration in educational standards that brought about Late Latin, a postclassical stage of the language seen in Christian writings of the time. It was more in line with the everyday speech not only because of a decline in education but also because of a desire to spread the word to the masses.</p>
<p>Despite the dialect variation (which is found in any widespread language) the languages of Spain, France, Portugal, and Italy retained a remarkable unity in phonological forms and developments, bolstered by the stabilising influence of their common Christian (Roman Catholic) culture. It was not until the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Moorish conquest of Spain</a> in 711 cut off communications between the major Romance regions that the languages began to diverge seriously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The Vulgar Latin dialect that would later become <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> diverged somewhat more from the other varieties, as it was largely cut off from the unifying influences in the western part of the Empire.</p>
<p>One way to determine whether a Romance language feature was in Vulgar Latin is to compare it with its parallel in Classical Latin. If it was not preferred in Classical Latin, then it most likely came from the invisible contemporaneous Vulgar Latin. For example, Romance "horse" (cavallo/cheval/caballo/cavalo) came from Latin <i>caballus</i>. However, Classical Latin used <i>equus</i>. <i>Caballus</i> therefore was most likely the spoken form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vulgar Latin began to diverge into distinct languages by the 9th century at the latest, when the earliest extant Romance writings begin to appear. They were, throughout the period, confined to everyday speech, as Medieval Latin was used for writing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Latin">Medieval Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Medieval Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1011" data-file-width="1500" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg/250px-Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg/375px-Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg/500px-Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Latin Malmesbury <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> from 1407.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Medieval Latin is the written Latin in use during that portion of the postclassical period when no corresponding Latin vernacular existed. The spoken language had developed into the various incipient Romance languages; however, in the educated and official world Latin continued without its natural spoken base. Moreover, this Latin spread into lands that had never spoken Latin, such as the Germanic and Slavic nations. It became useful for international communication between the member states of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and its allies.</p>
<p>Without the institutions of the Roman empire that had supported its uniformity, medieval Latin lost its linguistic cohesion: for example, in classical Latin <i>sum</i> and <i>eram</i> are used as auxiliary verbs in the perfect and pluperfect passive, which are compound tenses. Medieval Latin might use <i>fui</i> and <i>fueram</i> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorley13-15_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-thorley13-15-26">[26]</a></sup> Furthermore, the meanings of many words have been changed and new vocabularies have been introduced from the vernacular. Identifiable individual styles of classically incorrect Latin prevail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorley13-15_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-thorley13-15-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_Latin">Renaissance Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Renaissance Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Latin" title="Renaissance Latin">Renaissance Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="1394" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png/220px-Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png/330px-Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png/440px-Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Most 15th century printed books (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incunabula" title="Incunabula">incunabula</a>) were in Latin, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_language" title="Vernacular language">vernacular languages</a> playing only a secondary role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISTC_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISTC-27">[27]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> briefly reinforced the position of Latin as a spoken language by its adoption by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_Humanism" title="Renaissance Humanism">Renaissance Humanists</a>. Often led by members of the clergy, they were shocked by the accelerated dismantling of the vestiges of the classical world and the rapid loss of its literature. They strove to preserve what they could and restore Latin to what it had been and introduced the practice of producing revised editions of the literary works that remained by comparing surviving manuscripts. By no later than the 15th century they had replaced Medieval Latin with versions supported by the scholars of the rising universities, who attempted, by scholarship, to discover what the classical language had been.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Latin">New Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: New Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a></div>
<p>During the Early Modern Age, Latin still was the most important language of culture in Europe. Therefore, until the end of the 17th century the majority of books and almost all diplomatic documents were written in Latin. Afterwards, most diplomatic documents were written in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and later just native or other languages.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_Latin">Contemporary Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Contemporary Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Latin" title="Contemporary Latin">Contemporary Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/220px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/330px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/440px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The signs at <a href="/wiki/Wallsend_Metro_station" title="Wallsend Metro station">Wallsend Metro station</a> are in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and Latin as a tribute to Wallsend's role as one of the outposts of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The largest organisation that retains Latin in official and quasi-official contexts is the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>. Latin remains the language of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Tridentine_Mass" title="Tridentine Mass">Tridentine Mass</a> is celebrated in Latin. Although the <a href="/wiki/Mass_of_Paul_VI" title="Mass of Paul VI">Mass of Paul VI</a> is usually celebrated in the local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_language" title="Vernacular language">vernacular language</a>, it can be and often is said in Latin, in part or whole, especially at multilingual gatherings. It is the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>, the primary language of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_journal" title="Public journal">public journal</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Acta_Apostolicae_Sedis" title="Acta Apostolicae Sedis">Acta Apostolicae Sedis</a></i>, and the working language of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rota" title="Roman Rota">Roman Rota</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> is also home to the world's only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automatic_teller_machine" title="Automatic teller machine">automatic teller machine</a> that gives instructions in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_university" title="Pontifical university">pontifical universities</a> postgraduate courses of <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">Canon law</a> are taught in Latin, and papers are written in the same language.</p>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a>, after the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i> of 1559, a Latin edition was published in 1560 for use at universities such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford</a> and the leading "public schools" (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">English</a> private academies), where the liturgy was still permitted to be conducted in Latin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and there have been several Latin translations since. Most recently, a Latin edition of the 1979 USA Anglican Book of Common Prayer has appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some films of ancient settings, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sebastiane" title="Sebastiane">Sebastiane</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Passion_of_the_Christ" title="The Passion of the Christ">The Passion of the Christ</a></i>, have been made with dialogue in Latin for the sake of realism. Occasionally, Latin dialogue is used because of its association with religion or philosophy, in such film/<a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> series as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Exorcist_(film)" title="The Exorcist (film)">The Exorcist</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_(2004_TV_series)" title="Lost (2004 TV series)">Lost</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Jughead_(Lost)" title="Jughead (Lost)">Jughead</a>"). Subtitles are usually shown for the benefit of those who do not understand Latin. There are also <a href="/wiki/List_of_songs_with_Latin_lyrics" title="List of songs with Latin lyrics">songs written with Latin lyrics</a>. The libretto for the opera-oratorio <a href="/wiki/Oedipus_rex_(opera)" title="Oedipus rex (opera)"><i>Oedipus rex</i> (opera)</a> by <a href="/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky" title="Igor Stravinsky">Igor Stravinsky</a> is in Latin.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> adopts the country's Latin short name <i>Helvetia</i> on coins and stamps since there is no room to use all of the nation's <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Switzerland" title="Languages of Switzerland">four official languages</a>. For a similar reason, it adopted the international vehicle and internet code <i>CH</i>, which stands for <i>Confoederatio Helvetica</i>, the country's full Latin name.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="224" data-file-width="380" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg/220px-Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg/330px-Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg/440px-Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Council_of_the_EU_logo.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The polyglot <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> has adopted Latin names in the logos of some of its institutions for the sake of linguistic compromise, an "ecumenical nationalism" common to most of the continent and as a sign of the continent's heritage (such as the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">EU Council</a>: <i>Consilium</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many organizations today have Latin mottos, such as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semper_Paratus" title="Semper Paratus">Semper paratus</a>" (always ready), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a>, and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semper_Fidelis" title="Semper Fidelis">Semper fidelis</a>" (always faithful), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a>. Several of the states of the United States also have Latin mottos, such as "Qui transtulit sustinet" ("He who transplanted still sustains"), the state motto of <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>; "<a href="/wiki/Per_aspera_ad_astra" title="Per aspera ad astra">Ad astra per aspera</a>" ("To the stars through hardships"), that of <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>; "Si quaeris peninsulam amoenam, circumspice" ("If you seek a pleasant peninsula, look about you"), that of <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>; "<a href="/wiki/Salus_populi_suprema_lex_esto" title="Salus populi suprema lex esto">Salus populi suprema lex esto</a>" ("The health of the people should be the highest law"), that of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>; "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esse_Quam_Videri" title="Esse Quam Videri">Esse quam videri</a>" (To be rather than to seem), that of <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>; "<a href="/wiki/Sic_semper_tyrannis" title="Sic semper tyrannis">Sic semper tyrannis</a>" (Thus always for tyrants), that of <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>; and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montani_Semper_Liberi" title="Montani Semper Liberi">Montani semper liberi</a>" (Mountaineers are always free), that of <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>. Another Latin motto is "<a href="/wiki/Per_ardua_ad_astra" title="Per ardua ad astra">Per ardua ad astra</a>" (Through adversity/struggle to the stars), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force (RAF)</a>. Some schools adopt Latin mottos, for example <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>'s motto is "<a href="/wiki/Veritas" title="Veritas">Veritas</a>" meaning (truth). Veritas was the goddess of truth, a daughter of Saturn, and the mother of Virtue.</p>
<p>Similarly <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>'s motto "A mari usque ad mare" (from sea to sea) and most provincial mottos are also in Latin (<a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>'s is Splendor Sine Occasu (splendor without diminishment)).</p>
<p>Occasionally, some media outlets broadcast in Latin, which is targeted at enthusiasts. Notable examples include <a href="/wiki/Radio_Bremen" title="Radio Bremen">Radio Bremen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YLE" title="YLE">YLE</a> radio in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, and Vatican Radio &amp; Television, all of which broadcast news segments and other material in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are many websites and forums maintained in Latin by enthusiasts. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_Wikipedia" title="Latin Wikipedia">Latin Wikipedia</a> has more than 100,000 articles written in Latin.</p>
<p>Latin is taught in many high schools, especially in Europe and the Americas. It is most common in British <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">public schools</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar schools</a>, the Italian <a href="/wiki/Liceo_classico" title="Liceo classico">liceo classico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liceo_scientifico" title="Liceo scientifico">liceo scientifico</a>, the German Humanistisches <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(Germany)" title="Gymnasium (Germany)">Gymnasium</a> and the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, it is taught in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_College" title="Baltimore City College">Baltimore City College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston_Latin_Academy" title="Boston Latin Academy">Boston Latin Academy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston_Latin_School" title="Boston Latin School">Boston Latin School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Latin_School" title="Brooklyn Latin School">Brooklyn Latin School</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_High_School_of_Philadelphia" title="Central High School of Philadelphia">Central High School of Philadelphia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_High_School_of_Boston" title="English High School of Boston">English High School of Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwell_High_School_(Massachusetts)" title="Norwell High School (Massachusetts)">Norwell High School (Massachusetts)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oak_Hall_School" title="Oak Hall School">Oak Hall School</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_spelling_and_pronunciation" title="Latin spelling and pronunciation">Latin spelling and pronunciation</a></div>
<p>No inherited verbal knowledge of the ancient pronunciation of Latin exists so it must be reconstructed. Among the data used for reconstruction are explicit statements about pronunciation by ancient authors, misspellings, puns, ancient etymologies, and the spelling of Latin loanwords in other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>In Old and Classical Latin, the Latin alphabet had no distinction between <a href="/wiki/Letter_case" title="Letter case">uppercase and lowercase</a>, and the letters ⟨J U W⟩ did not exist. In place of ⟨J U⟩, ⟨I V⟩ were used. ⟨I V⟩ represented both vowels and consonants. Most of the letterforms were similar to modern uppercase, as can be seen in the inscription from the Colosseum shown at the top of the article.</p>
<p>The spelling systems used in Latin dictionaries and modern editions of Latin texts, however, normally use ⟨i u⟩ in place of Classical-era ⟨i v⟩. Some systems use ⟨j v⟩ for the consonant sounds <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j w/</span> except in the combinations ⟨gu su qu⟩ for which ⟨v⟩ is never used.</p>
<p>Some notes concerning the mapping of Latin phonemes to English graphemes are given below:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Notes</caption>
<tr>
<th>Latin<br/>
grapheme</th>
<th>Latin<br/>
phone</th>
<th>English examples</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨c⟩, ⟨k⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[k]</span></th>
<td>Always hard as <i>k</i> in <i>sky</i>, never <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_C" title="Hard and soft C">soft</a> as in <i>central</i>, <i>cello</i>, or <i>social</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨t⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t]</span></th>
<td>As <i>t</i> in <i>stay</i>, never as <i>t</i> in <i>nation</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨s⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s]</span></th>
<td>As <i>s</i> in <i>say</i>, never as <i>s</i> in <i>rise</i> or <i>issue</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨g⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡ]</span></th>
<td>Always hard as <i>g</i> in <i>good</i>, never <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_G" title="Hard and soft G">soft</a> as <i>g</i> in <i>gem</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ŋ]</span></th>
<td>Before ⟨n⟩, as <i>ng</i> in <i>sing</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨n⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[n]</span></th>
<td>As <i>n</i> in <i>man</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ŋ]</span></th>
<td>Before ⟨c⟩, ⟨x⟩, and ⟨g⟩, as <i>ng</i> in <i>sing</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨l⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[l]</span></th>
<td>When doubled ⟨ll⟩ and before ⟨i⟩, as clear <i>l</i> in <i>link</i> (<i>l exilis</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɫ]</span></th>
<td>In all other positions, as dark <i>l</i> in <i>bowl</i> (<i>l pinguis</i>)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨qu⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kʷ]</span></th>
<td>Similar to <i>qu</i> in <i>quick</i>, never as <i>qu</i> in <i>antique</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨u⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[w]</span></th>
<td>Sometimes at the beginning of a syllable, or after ⟨g⟩ and ⟨s⟩, as <i>w</i> in <i>wine</i>, never as <i>v</i> in <i>vine</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨i⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[j]</span></th>
<td>Sometimes at the beginning of a syllable, as <i>y</i> in <i>yard</i>, never as <i>j</i> in <i>just</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[jj]</span></th>
<td>Doubled between vowels, as <i>y y</i> in <i>toy yacht</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨x⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ks]</span></th>
<td>A letter representing ⟨c⟩ + ⟨s⟩: as <i>x</i> in English <i>axe</i>, never as <i>x</i> in <i>example</i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">Doubled</a> consonants in Latin are pronounced long. In English, consonants are pronounced double only between two words or <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>, as in <i>unnamed</i>, which has a doubled <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/nn/</span> like the <i>nn</i> in Latin <i>annus</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_vowels">Simple vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Simple vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Front</th>
<th>Central</th>
<th>Back</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Close</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">iː ɪ</span></td>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʊ uː</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Mid</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">eː ɛ</span></td>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɔ oː</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Open</th>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">a aː</span></td>
<td></td>
</tr>

<p>In Classical Latin, the letter ⟨U⟩ was written as ⟨V⟩ even if it was used as a vowel. ⟨Y⟩ was adopted to represent <a href="/wiki/Upsilon" title="Upsilon">upsilon</a> in loanwords from Greek, but it was pronounced like ⟨u⟩ and ⟨i⟩ by some speakers. It was also used in native Latin words by confusion with Greek words of similar meaning, such as sylva and ὕλη.</p>
<p>Classical Latin distinguished between <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long and short vowels</a>. Then, long vowels, except for ⟨I⟩, were frequently marked using the <a href="/wiki/Apex_(diacritic)" title="Apex (diacritic)">apex</a>, which was sometimes similar to an <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> ⟨Á É Ó V́ Ý⟩. Long <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/iː/</span> was written using a taller version of ⟨I⟩, called <i>i longa</i> "long I": ⟨ꟾ⟩. In modern texts, long vowels are often indicated by a <a href="/wiki/Macron" title="Macron">macron</a> ⟨ā ē ī ō ū⟩, and short vowels are usually unmarked except when it is necessary to distinguish between words, when they are marked with a <a href="/wiki/Breve" title="Breve">breve</a>: ⟨ă ĕ ĭ ŏ ŭ⟩.</p>
<p>Long vowels in Classical Latin were pronounced with a different quality from short vowels and also were longer. The difference is described in table below:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Pronunciation of Latin vowels</caption>
<tr>
<th>Latin<br/>
grapheme</th>
<th>Latin<br/>
phone</th>
<th>modern examples</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨a⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[a]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>u</i> in <i>cut</i> when short</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aː]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>a</i> in <i>father</i> when long</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨e⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛ]</span></th>
<td>as <i>e</i> in <i>pet</i> when short</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[eː]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>ey</i> in <i>they</i> when long</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨i⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪ]</span></th>
<td>as <i>i</i> in <i>sit</i> when short</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[iː]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>i</i> in <i>machine</i> when long</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨o⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɔ]</span></th>
<td>as <i>o</i> in <i>sort</i> when short</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oː]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>o</i> in <i>holy</i> when long</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨u⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊ]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>u</i> in <i>put</i> when short</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[uː]</span></th>
<td>similar to <i>u</i> in <i>true</i> when long</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨y⟩</th>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʏ]</span></th>
<td>as in German <i>Stück</i> when short (or as short <i>u</i> or <i>i</i>)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[yː]</span></th>
<td>as in German <i>früh</i> when long (or as long <i>u</i> or <i>i</i>)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A vowel and ⟨m⟩ at the end of a word, or a vowel and ⟨n⟩ before ⟨s⟩ or ⟨f⟩, was long and <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">nasal</a>, as in <i>monstrum</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/mõːstrũː/</span>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diphthongs">Diphthongs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Diphthongs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Classical Latin had several <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a>. The two most common were ⟨ae au⟩. ⟨oe⟩ was fairly rare, and ⟨ui eu ei ou⟩ were very rare, at least in native Latin words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classical_diphthongs_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-classical_diphthongs-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The sequences sometimes did not represent diphthongs. ⟨ae⟩ and ⟨oe⟩ also represented a sequence of two vowels in different syllables in <i>aēnus</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aˈeː.nʊs]</span> "of bronze" and <i>coēpit</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kɔˈeː.pɪt]</span> "began", and ⟨au ui eu ei ou⟩ represented sequences of two vowels or of a vowel and one of the semivowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j w/</span>, in <i>cavē</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈka.weː]</span> "beware!", <i>cuius</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈkʊj.jʊs]</span> "whose", <i>monuī</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈmɔn.ʊ.iː]</span> "I warned", <i>solvī</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈsɔɫ.wiː]</span> "I released", <i>dēlēvī</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[deːˈleː.wiː]</span> "I destroyed", <i>eius</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɛj.jʊs]</span> "his", and <i>novus</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈnɔ.wʊs]</span> "new".</p>
<p>Old Latin had more diphthongs, but most of them changed into long vowels in Classical Latin. The Old Latin diphthong ⟨ai⟩ and the sequence ⟨āī⟩ became Classical ⟨ae⟩. Old Latin ⟨oi⟩ and ⟨ou⟩ changed to Classical ⟨ū⟩, except in a few words whose ⟨oi⟩ became Classical ⟨oe⟩. These two developments sometimes occurred in different words from the same root: for instance, Classical <i>poena</i> "punishment" and <i>pūnīre</i> "to punish".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classical_diphthongs_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-classical_diphthongs-36">[36]</a></sup> Early Old Latin ⟨ei⟩ usually changed to Classical ⟨ī⟩.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Vulgar Latin and the Romance languages, ⟨ae au oe⟩ merged with ⟨e ō ē⟩. A similar pronunciation also existed during the Classical Latin period for less-educated speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classical_diphthongs_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-classical_diphthongs-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">

<tr>
<th></th>

<th>Back</th>
</tr>

</tr>

<td>ei <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ei̯/</span><br/>
