<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duenos_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="460" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Duenos_inscription.jpg/220px-Duenos_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Duenos_inscription.jpg/330px-Duenos_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Duenos_inscription.jpg/440px-Duenos_inscription.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duenos_inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Duenos_Inscription" title="Duenos Inscription">Duenos Inscription</a>, from the 6th century BC, is one of the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> texts.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Latin was written in the Latin alphabet, derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Italic_script" title="Old Italic script">Old Italic script</a>, which was in turn drawn from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> and ultimately the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This alphabet has continued to be used over the centuries as the script for the Romance, Celtic, Germanic, Baltic, Finnic, and many Slavic languages (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>); and it has been adopted by many languages around the world, including <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a>, and most languages in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, making it by far the world's single most widely used writing system.</p>
<p>The number of letters in the Latin alphabet has varied. When it was first derived from the Etruscan alphabet, it contained only 21 letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Later, <i>G</i> was added to represent <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span>, which had previously been spelled <i>C</i>, and <i>Z</i> ceased to be included in the alphabet, as the language then had no <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">voiced alveolar fricative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D538_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-D538-40">[40]</a></sup> The letters <i>Y</i> and <i>Z</i> were later added to represent Greek letters, <a href="/wiki/Upsilon" title="Upsilon">upsilon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeta" title="Zeta">zeta</a> respectively, in Greek loanwords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D538_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-D538-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>W</i> was created in the 11th century from <i>VV</i>. It represented <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> in Germanic languages, not Latin, which still uses <i>V</i> for the purpose. <i>J</i> was distinguished from the original <i>I</i> only during the late Middle Ages, as was the letter <i>U</i> from <i>V</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D538_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-D538-40">[40]</a></sup> Although some Latin dictionaries use <i>J</i>, it is rarely used for Latin text, as it was not used in classical times, but many other languages use it.</p>
<p>Classical Latin did not contain sentence <a href="/wiki/Punctuation" title="Punctuation">punctuation</a>, letter case,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interword_spacing" title="Interword spacing">interword spacing</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Apex_(diacritic)" title="Apex (diacritic)">apices</a> were sometimes used to distinguish length in vowels and the <a href="/wiki/Interpunct" title="Interpunct">interpunct</a> was used at times to separate words. The first line of Catullus 3, originally written as</p>
<dl>
<dd>LV́GÉTEÓVENERÉSCVPÍDINÉSQVE ("Mourn, O <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venuses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupids</a>")</dd>
</dl>
<p>or with interpunct as</p>
<dl>
<dd>LV́GÉTE·Ó·VENERÉS·CVPÍDINÉSQVE</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>
</ul>
<p>would be rendered in a modern edition as</p>
<dl>
<dd>Lugete, o Veneres Cupidinesque</dd>
</dl>
<p>or with macrons</p>
<dl>
<dd>Lūgēte, ō Venerēs Cupīdinēsque.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hocgracili.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="182" data-file-width="691" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hocgracili.jpg/250px-Hocgracili.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hocgracili.jpg/375px-Hocgracili.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hocgracili.jpg/500px-Hocgracili.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hocgracili.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A replica of the Old Roman Cursive inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Vindolanda_tablets" title="Vindolanda tablets">Vindolanda tablets</a>, the oldest surviving handwritten documents in Britain.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_cursive" title="Roman cursive">Roman cursive</a> script is commonly found on the many <a href="/wiki/Wax_tablet" title="Wax tablet">wax tablets</a> excavated at sites such as forts, an especially extensive set having been discovered at Vindolanda on <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>. Curiously enough, most of the <a href="/wiki/Vindolanda_tablets" title="Vindolanda tablets">Vindolanda tablets</a> show spaces between words, but spaces were avoided in monumental inscriptions from that era.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternate_scripts">Alternate scripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Alternate scripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Occasionally, Latin has been written in other scripts:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Praeneste_fibula" title="Praeneste fibula">Praeneste fibula</a> is a 7th-century BC pin with an Old Latin inscription written using the Etruscan script.</li>
<li>The rear panel of the early 8th-century <a href="/wiki/Franks_Casket" title="Franks Casket">Franks Casket</a> has an inscription that switches from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_runes" title="Anglo-Saxon runes">Anglo-Saxon runes</a> to Latin in Latin script and to Latin in runes.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Latin_grammar" title="Latin grammar">Latin grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_syntax" title="Latin syntax">Latin syntax</a></div>
<p>Latin is a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">fusional language</a> in the terminology of linguistic typology. In more traditional terminology, it is an inflected language, but typologists are apt to say "inflecting". Words include an objective semantic element and markers specifying the grammatical use of the word. The fusion of root meaning and markers produces very compact sentence elements: <i>amō</i>, "I love," is produced from a semantic element, <i>ama-</i>, "love," to which <i>-ō</i>, a first person singular marker, is suffixed.</p>
<p>The grammatical function can be changed by changing the markers: the word is "inflected" to express different grammatical functions, but the semantic element does not change. (Inflection uses affixing and infixing. Affixing is prefixing and suffixing. Latin inflections are never prefixed.)</p>
<p>For example, <i>amābit</i>, "he or she or it will love", is formed from the same stem, <i>amā-</i>, to which a future tense marker, <i>-bi-</i>, is suffixed, and a third person singular marker, <i>-t</i>, is suffixed. There is an inherent ambiguity: <i>-t</i> may denote more than one grammatical category: masculine, feminine, or neuter gender. A major task in understanding Latin phrases and clauses is to clarify such ambiguities by an analysis of context. All natural languages contain ambiguities of one sort or another.</p>
<p>The inflections express <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a> in <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a>, a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">declension</a></i>. Markers are also attached to fixed stems of verbs, to denote <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a>, number, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" title="Grammatical voice">voice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">mood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a>, a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugation</a></i>. Some words are uninflected and undergo neither process, such as adverbs, prepositions, and interjections.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">Nouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nouns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension" title="Latin declension">Latin declension</a></div>
<p>A regular Latin noun belongs to one of five main declensions, a group of nouns with similar inflected forms. The declensions are identified by the genitive singular form of the noun. The first declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>a</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-ae</i>. The second declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>o</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-i</i>. The third declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>i</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-is</i>. The fourth declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>u</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-ūs</i>. The fifth declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>e</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-ei</i>.</p>
<p>There are seven Latin noun cases, which also apply to adjectives and pronouns and mark a noun's syntactic role in the sentence by means of inflections. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> is not as important in Latin as it is in English, which is less inflected. The general structure and word order of a Latin sentence can therefore vary. The cases are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">Nominative</a></b> – used when the noun is the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predicate_nominative" title="Predicate nominative">predicate nominative</a>. The thing or person acting: the <b>girl</b> ran: <i><b>puella</b> cucurrit,</i> or <i>cucurrit <b>puella</b></i></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">Genitive</a></b> – used when the noun is the possessor of or connected with an object: "the horse of the man", or "the man's horse"; in both instances, the word <i>man</i> would be in the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a> when it is translated into Latin). It also indicates the <a href="/wiki/Partitive" title="Partitive">partitive</a>, in which the material is quantified: "a group of people"; "a number of gifts": <i>people</i> and <i>gifts</i> would be in the genitive case). Some nouns are genitive with special verbs and adjectives: The cup is full of <b>wine</b>. <i>Poculum plēnum <b>vīnī</b> est.</i> The master of the <b>slave</b> had beaten him. <i>Dominus <b>servī</b> eum verberāverat.</i></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">Dative</a></b> – used when the noun is the indirect object of the sentence, with special verbs, with certain prepositions, and if it is used as agent, reference, or even possessor: The merchant hands the <a href="/wiki/Stola" title="Stola">stola</a> <b>to the woman</b>. <i>Mercātor <b>fēminae</b> stolam trādit.</i>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">Accusative</a></b> – used when the noun is the direct object of the subject and as the object of a preposition demonstrating place to which.: The man killed <b>the boy</b>. <i>Vir necāvit <b>puerum</b>.</i></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">Ablative</a></b> – used when the noun demonstrates separation or movement from a source, cause, <a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrument</a> or when the noun is used as the object of certain prepositions; adverbial: You walked <b>with the boy</b>. <i>Cum <b>puerō</b> ambulāvistī.</i></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">Vocative</a></b> – used when the noun is used in a direct address. The vocative form of a noun is often the same as the nominative, but exceptions include second-declension nouns ending in <i>-us</i>. The <i>-us</i> becomes an <i>-e</i> in the vocative singular. If it ends in <i>-ius</i> (such as <i>fīlius</i>), the ending is just <i>-ī</i> (<i>filī</i>), as distinct from the nominative plural (<i>filiī</i>) in the vocative singular: "<b>Master</b>!" shouted the slave. <i>"<b>Domine</b>!" clāmāvit servus.</i></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">Locative</a></b> – used to indicate a location (corresponding to the English "in" or "at"). It is far less common than the other six cases of Latin nouns and usually applies to cities and small towns and islands along with a few common nouns, such as the word <i>domus</i> (house). In the singular of the first and second declensions, its form coincides with the genitive (<i>Roma</i> becomes <i>Romae</i>, "in Rome"). In the plural of all declensions and the singular of the other declensions, it coincides with the ablative (<i>Athēnae</i> becomes <i>Athēnīs</i>, "at Athens"). In the fourth-declension word <i>domus</i>, the locative form, <i>domī</i> ("at home") differs from the standard form of all other cases.</li>
</ol>
<p>Latin lacks both definite and indefinite <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">articles</a> so <i>puer currit</i> can mean either "the boy is running" or "a boy is running".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">Adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension" title="Latin declension">Latin declension</a></div>
<p>There are two types of regular Latin adjectives: first- and second- declension and third-declension. They are so-called because their forms are similar or identical to first- and second-declension and third-declension nouns, respectively. Latin adjectives also have comparative (more --, <i>-er</i>) and superlative (most --, <i>est</i>) forms. There are also a number of Latin participles.</p>
<p>Latin numbers are sometimes declined. See <i>Numbers</i> below.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First-_and_second-declension_adjectives">First- and second-declension adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: First- and second-declension adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>First- and second-declension adjectives are declined like first-declension nouns for the feminine forms and like second-declension nouns for the masculine and neuter forms. For example, for <i>mortuus, mortua, mortuum</i> (dead), <i>mortua</i> is declined like a regular first-declension noun (such as <i>puella</i> (girl)), <i>mortuus</i> is declined like a regular second-declension masculine noun (such as <i>dominus</i> (lord, master)), and <i>mortuum</i> is declined like a regular second-declension neuter noun (such as <i>auxilium</i> (help)).</p>
<p><b><small>First- and second-declension <i>-er</i> adjectives</small></b></p>
<p>Some first- and second-declension adjectives have an <i>-er</i> as the masculine nominative singular form and are declined like regular first- and second-declension adjectives. Some but not all adjectives keep the <i>e</i> for all of the forms.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Third-declension_adjectives">Third-declension adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Third-declension adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Third-declension adjectives are mostly declined like normal third-declension nouns, with a few exceptions. In the plural nominative neuter, for example, the ending is <i>-ia</i> (<i>omnia</i> (all, everything)), and for third-declension nouns, the plural nominative neuter ending is <i>-a</i> or <i>-ia</i> (<i>capita</i> (heads), <i>animalia</i> (animals)) They can have one, two or three forms for the masculine, feminine, and neuter nominative singular.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Participles">Participles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Participles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Latin participles, like English participles, are formed from a verb. There are a few main types of participles: Present Active Participles, Perfect Passive Participles, Future Active Participles, and Future Passive Participles.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prepositions">Prepositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Prepositions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin sometimes uses prepositions, depending on the type of prepositional phrase being used. Prepositions can take two cases for their object: the accusative ("apud puerum" (with the boy), with "puerum" being the accusative form of "puer", boy) and the ablative ("sine puero" (without the boy), "puero" being the ablative form of "puer", boy).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Verbs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_conjugation" title="Latin conjugation">Latin conjugation</a></div>
<p>A regular verb in Latin belongs to one of four main <a href="/wiki/Latin_conjugation" title="Latin conjugation">conjugations</a>. A conjugation is "a class of verbs with similar inflected forms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The conjugations are identified by the last letter of the verb's present stem. The present stem can be found by omitting the -<i>re</i> (-<i>rī</i> in deponent verbs) ending from the present infinitive form. The infinitive of the first conjugation ends in <i>-ā-re</i> or <i>-ā-ri</i> (active and passive respectively): <i>amāre</i>, "to love," <i>hortārī</i>, "to exhort"; of the second conjugation by <i>-ē-re</i> or <i>-ē-rī</i>: <i>monēre</i>, "to warn", <i>verērī</i>, "to fear;" of the third conjugation by <i>-ere</i>, <i>-ī</i>: <i>dūcere</i>, "to lead," <i>ūtī</i>, "to use"; of the fourth by <i>-ī-re</i>, <i>-ī-rī</i>: <i>audīre</i>, "to hear," <i>experīrī</i>, "to attempt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_2011_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_2011-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Irregular verbs may not follow the types or may be marked in a different way. The "endings" presented above are not the suffixed infinitive markers. The first letter in each case is the last of the stem so the conjugations are also called a-conjugation, e-conjugation and i-conjugation. The fused infinitive ending is -<i>re</i> or -<i>rī</i>. Third-conjugation stems end in a consonant: the consonant conjugation. Further, there is a subset of the third conjugation, the i-stems, which behave somewhat like the fourth conjugation, as they are both i-stems, one short and the other long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_2011_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_2011-43">[43]</a></sup> The stem categories descend from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> and can therefore be compared to similar conjugations in other Indo-European languages.</p>
<p>There are six general <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a> in Latin (present, imperfect, future, perfect, pluperfect and future perfect), three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">moods</a> (indicative, imperative and subjunctive, in addition to the <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">gerund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerundive" title="Gerundive">gerundive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supine" title="Supine">supine</a>), three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">persons</a> (first, second and third), two numbers (singular and plural), two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" title="Grammatical voice">voices</a> (active and passive) and three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspects</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfective_and_imperfective" title="Perfective and imperfective">perfective, imperfective</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stative_verb" title="Stative verb">stative</a>). Verbs are described by four principal parts:</p>
<ol>
<li>The first principal part is the first-person singular, present tense, indicative mood, active voice form of the verb. If the verb is impersonal, the first principal part will be in the third-person singular.</li>
<li>The second principal part is the present infinitive active.</li>
<li>The third principal part is the first-person singular, perfect indicative active form. Like the first principal part, if the verb is impersonal, the third principal part will be in the third-person singular.</li>
<li>The fourth principal part is the supine form, or alternatively, the nominative singular, perfect passive participle form of the verb. The fourth principal part can show one gender of the participle or all three genders (-<i>us</i> for masculine, -<i>a</i> for feminine and -<i>m</i> for neuter) in the nominative singular. The fourth principal part will be the future participle if the verb cannot be made passive. Most modern Latin dictionaries, if they show only one gender, tend to show the masculine; but many older dictionaries instead show the neuter, as it coincides with the supine. The fourth principal part is sometimes omitted for intransitive verbs, but strictly in Latin, they can be made passive if they are used impersonally, and the supine exists for such verbs.</li>
</ol>
<p>There are six tenses in the Latin language. These are divided into two tense systems: the present system, which is made up of the present, imperfect and future tenses, and the perfect system, which is made up of the perfect, pluperfect and future perfect tenses. Each tense has a set of endings corresponding to the person and number referred to. Subject (nominative) pronouns are generally omitted for the first (<i>I, we</i>) and second (<i>you</i>) persons unless emphasis on the subject is desired.</p>
<p>The table below displays the common inflected endings for the indicative mood in the active voice in all six tenses. For the future tense, the first listed endings are for the first and second conjugations, and the second listed endings are for the third and fourth conjugations:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Tense</th>

<th>3rd-Person Singular</th>

<th>2nd-Person Plural</th>
<th>3rd-Person Plural</th>
</tr>

<td>Present</td>
<td>-ō/m</td>
<td>-s</td>
<td>-t</td>
<td>-mus</td>

<p>The future perfect endings are identical to the future forms of <i>sum</i> (with the exception of <i>erint</i>) and that the pluperfect endings are identical to the imperfect forms of <i>sum</i>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deponent_verbs">Deponent verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Deponent verbs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some Latin verbs are <a href="/wiki/Deponent_verb" title="Deponent verb">deponent</a>, causing their forms to be in the passive voice but retain an active meaning: <i>hortor, hortārī, hortātus sum (to urge).</i></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As Latin is an Italic language, most of its vocabulary is likewise Italic, ultimately from the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a>. However, because of close cultural interaction, the Romans not only adapted the Etruscan alphabet to form the Latin alphabet but also borrowed some <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a> words into their language, including <i>persona</i> "mask" and <i>histrio</i> "actor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26S13_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-H.26S13-44">[44]</a></sup> Latin also included vocabulary borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Oscan_language" title="Oscan language">Oscan</a>, another Italic language.</p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taranto" title="History of Taranto">Fall of Tarentum</a> (272 BC), the Romans began hellenizing, or adopting features of Greek culture, including the borrowing of Greek words, such as <i>camera</i> (vaulted roof), <i>sumbolum</i> (symbol), and <i>balineum</i> (bath).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26S13_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-H.26S13-44">[44]</a></sup> This hellenization led to the addition of "Y" and "Z" to the alphabet to represent Greek sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Subsequently the Romans transplanted <a href="/wiki/Greek_art" title="Greek art">Greek art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> to Italy, paying almost any price to entice Greek skilled and educated persons to Rome and sending their youth to be educated in Greece. Thus, many Latin scientific and philosophical words were Greek loanwords or had their meanings expanded by association with Greek words, as <i>ars</i> (craft) and τέχνη (art).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26S14_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-H.26S14-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of the Roman Empire's expansion and subsequent trade with outlying European tribes, the Romans borrowed some northern and central European words, such as <i>beber</i> (beaver), of Germanic origin, and <i>bracae</i> (breeches), of Celtic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.26S14_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-H.26S14-46">[46]</a></sup> The specific dialects of Latin across Latin-speaking regions of the former Roman Empire after its fall were influenced by languages specific to the regions. The dialects of Latin evolved into different Romance languages.</p>
<p>During and after the adoption of Christianity into Roman society, Christian vocabulary became a part of the language, either from Greek or Hebrew borrowings or as Latin neologisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Continuing into the Middle Ages, Latin incorporated many more words from surrounding languages, including <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>.</p>
<p>Over the ages, Latin-speaking populations produced new adjectives, nouns, and verbs by <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounding</a> meaningful <a href="/wiki/Segment_(linguistics)" title="Segment (linguistics)">segments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> For example, the compound adjective, <i>omnipotens</i>, "all-powerful," was produced from the adjectives <i>omnis</i>, "all", and <i>potens</i>, "powerful", by dropping the final <i>s</i> of <i>omnis</i> and concatenating. Often, the concatenation changed the part of speech, and nouns were produced from verb segments or verbs from nouns and adjectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phrases">Phrases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Phrases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The phrases are mentioned with <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">accents</a> to show where stress is placed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In Latin, most words are stressed at the second-last (penultimate) <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a>, called in Latin <i>paenultima</i> or <i>syllaba paenultima</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LKHS_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-LKHS-51">[51]</a></sup> A few words are stressed at the third-last syllable, called in Latin <i>antepaenultima</i> or <i>syllaba antepaenultima</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LKHS_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-LKHS-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>sálve</b> <small>to one person</small> / <b>salvéte</b> <small>to more than one person</small> - hello</p>
<p><b>áve</b> <small>to one person</small> / <b>avéte</b> <small>to more than one person</small> - greetings</p>
<p><b>vále</b> <small>to one person</small> / <b>valéte</b> <small>to more than one person</small> - goodbye</p>
<p><b>cúra ut váleas</b> - take care</p>
<p><b>exoptátus</b> <small>to male</small> / <b>exoptáta</b> <small>to female</small>, <b>optátus</b> <small>to male</small> / <b>optáta</b> <small>to female</small>, <b>grátus</b> <small>to male</small> / <b>gráta</b> <small>to female</small>, <b>accéptus</b> <small>to male</small> / <b>accépta</b> <small>to female</small> - welcome</p>
<p><b>quómodo váles?</b>, <b>ut váles?</b> - how are you?</p>
<p><b>béne</b> - good</p>
<p><b>amabo te</b> - please</p>
<p><b>béne váleo</b> - I'm fine</p>
<p><b>mále</b> - bad</p>
<p><b>mále váleo</b> - I'm not good</p>
<p><b>quáeso</b> (['kwajso]/['kwe:so]) - please</p>
<p><b>íta</b>, <b>íta est</b>, <b>íta véro</b>, <b>sic</b>, <b>sic est</b>, <b>étiam</b> - yes</p>
<p><b>non</b>, <b>minime</b> - no</p>
<p><b>grátias tíbi</b>, <b>grátias tíbi ágo</b> - thank you</p>
<p><b>mágnas grátias</b>, <b>mágnas grátias ágo</b> - many thanks</p>
<p><b>máximas grátias</b>, <b>máximas grátias ágo</b>, <b>ingéntes grátias ágo</b> - thank you very much</p>
<p><b>accípe sis</b> <small>to one person</small> / <b>accípite sítis</b> <small>to more than one person</small>, <b>libénter</b> - you're welcome</p>
<p><b>qua aetáte es?</b> - how old are you?</p>
<p><b>25 ánnos nátus</b> <small>to male</small> / <b>25 ánnos náta</b> <small>to female</small> - 25 years old</p>
<p><b>loquerísne ...</b> - do you speak ...</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Latíne?</b> - Latin?</li>
<li><b>Gráece?</b> (['grajke]/['gre:ke]) - Greek?</li>
<li><b>Ánglice?</b> (['aŋlike]) - English?</li>
<li><b>Italiáne?</b> - Italian?</li>
<li><b>Gallice?</b> - French?</li>
<li><b>Hispánice?</b> - Spanish?</li>
<li><b>Lusitánice?</b> - Portuguese?</li>
<li><b>Theodísce?</b> ([teo'diske]) - German?</li>
<li><b>Sínice?</b> - Chinese?</li>
<li><b>Japónice?</b> ([ja'po:nike]) - Japanese?</li>
<li><b>Coreane?</b> - Korean?</li>
<li><b>Arábice?</b> - Arabic?</li>
<li><b>Pérsice?</b> - Persian?</li>
<li><b>Indice?</b> - Hindi?</li>
<li><b>Rússice?</b> - Russian?</li>
</ul>
<p><b>úbi latrína est?</b> - where is the toilet?</p>
<p><b>ámo te</b> / <b>te ámo</b> - I love you</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers">Numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In ancient times, numbers in Latin were written only with letters. Today, the numbers can be written with the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numbers</a> as well as with <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">Roman numerals</a>. The numbers 1, 2 and 3 and every whole hundred from 200 to 900 are declined as nouns and adjectives, with some differences.</p>
<table>
<tr>
<td><i>ūnus, ūna, ūnum</i> (masculine, feminine, neuter)</td>
<td></td>
<td>I</td>
<td></td>
<td>one</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>duo, duae, duo</i> (m., f., n.)</td>
<td></td>

<td></td>
<td>two</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>trēs, tria</i> (m./f., n.)</td>
<td></td>
<td>III</td>
<td></td>
<td>three</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>quattuor</i></td>
<td></td>
<td>IIII <small>or</small> IV</td>
<td></td>
<td>four</td>
</tr>
<tr>

<td></td>
<td>one thousand</td>
</tr>

<p>The numbers from 4 to 100 often do not change their endings.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_text">Example text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Example text">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i>, also called <i>De Bello Gallico</i> (<i>The Gallic War</i>), written by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Gaius Julius Caesar</a>, begins with the following passage:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Gallia est omnis divisa in partes tres, quarum unam incolunt Belgae, aliam Aquitani, tertiam qui ipsorum lingua Celtae, nostra Galli appellantur. Hi omnes lingua, institutis, legibus inter se differunt. Gallos ab Aquitanis Garumna flumen, a Belgis Matrona et Sequana dividit. Horum omnium fortissimi sunt Belgae, propterea quod a cultu atque humanitate provinciae longissime absunt, minimeque ad eos mercatores saepe commeant atque ea quae ad effeminandos animos pertinent important, proximique sunt Germanis, qui trans Rhenum incolunt, quibuscum continenter bellum gerunt. Qua de causa Helvetii quoque reliquos Gallos virtute praecedunt, quod fere cotidianis proeliis cum Germanis contendunt, cum aut suis finibus eos prohibent aut ipsi in eorum finibus bellum gerunt. Eorum una pars, quam Gallos obtinere dictum est, initium capit a flumine Rhodano, continetur Garumna flumine, Oceano, finibus Belgarum; attingit etiam ab Sequanis et Helvetiis flumen Rhenum; vergit ad septentriones. Belgae ab extremis Galliae finibus oriuntur; pertinent ad inferiorem partem fluminis Rheni; spectant in septentrionem et orientem solem. Aquitania a Garumna flumine ad Pyrenaeos montes et eam partem Oceani quae est ad Hispaniam pertinet; spectat inter occasum solis et septentriones.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_tools">Language tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Language tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/resolveform?lang=la" rel="nofollow">"Latin Dictionary Headword Search"</a>. <i>Perseus Hopper</i>. Tufts University.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.atitle=Latin+Dictionary+Headword+Search&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu%2Fhopper%2Fresolveform%3Flang%3Dla&amp;rft.jtitle=Perseus+Hopper&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Searches Lewis &amp; Short's <i>A Latin Dictionary</i> and Lewis's <i>An Elementary Latin Dictionary</i>. Online results.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.online-latin-dictionary.com" rel="nofollow">"Online Latin Dictionary with conjugator and declension tool"</a>. Olivetti Media Communication.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.btitle=Online+Latin+Dictionary+with+conjugator+and+declension+tool&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.online-latin-dictionary.com&amp;rft.pub=Olivetti+Media+Communication&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Search on line Latin-English and English-Latin dictionary with complete declension or conjugation. Online results.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/morph?lang=la" rel="nofollow">"Latin Word Study Tool"</a>. <i>Perseus Hopper</i>. Tufts University.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.atitle=Latin+Word+Study+Tool&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu%2Fhopper%2Fmorph%3Flang%3Dla&amp;rft.jtitle=Perseus+Hopper&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Identifies the grammatical functions of words entered. Online results.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web">Aversa, Alan. <a class="external text" href="http://www.u.arizona.edu/~aversa/latin/" rel="nofollow">"Latin Inflector"</a>. University of Arizona.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.aufirst=Alan&amp;rft.aulast=Aversa&amp;rft.btitle=Latin+Inflector&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.u.arizona.edu%2F~aversa%2Flatin%2F&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Arizona&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Identifies the grammatical functions of all the words in sentences entered, using Perseus.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.verbix.com/languages/latin.shtml" rel="nofollow">"Latin Verb Conjugator"</a>. Verbix.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.btitle=Latin+Verb+Conjugator&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.verbix.com%2Flanguages%2Flatin.shtml&amp;rft.pub=Verbix&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Displays complete conjugations of verbs entered in first-person present singular form.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://stranica.net63.net/verba_latina" rel="nofollow">"Online Latin Verb Conjugator"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.btitle=Online+Latin+Verb+Conjugator&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fstranica.net63.net%2Fverba_latina&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Displays conjugation of verbs entered in their infinitive form.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web">Whittaker, William. <a class="external text" href="http://lysy2.archives.nd.edu/cgi-bin/words.exe" rel="nofollow">"Words"</a>. Notre Dame Archives.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.aufirst=William&amp;rft.aulast=Whittaker&amp;rft.btitle=Words&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Flysy2.archives.nd.edu%2Fcgi-bin%2Fwords.exe&amp;rft.pub=Notre+Dame+Archives&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Identifies Latin words entered. Translates English words entered.</li>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://alpheios.net" rel="nofollow">"Alpheios"</a>. Alpheios Project.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.btitle=Alpheios&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Falpheios.net&amp;rft.pub=Alpheios+Project&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Combines Whittakers Words, Lewis and Short, Bennett's grammar and inflection tables in a browser addon.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.dmoz.org/Reference/Dictionaries/World_Languages/L/Latin/" rel="nofollow">Latin Dictionaries</a> at <a href="/wiki/DMOZ" title="DMOZ">DMOZ</a></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Dymock, John (1830). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=2sIDAAAAQAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>A new abridgment of Ainsworth's Dictionary, English and Latin, for the use of Grammar Schools</i></a> (4th ed.). Glasgow: Hutchison &amp; Brookman.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALatin&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.aulast=Dymock&amp;rft.btitle=A+new+abridgment+of+Ainsworth%27s+Dictionary%2C+English+and+Latin%2C+for+the+use+of+Grammar+Schools&amp;rft.date=1830&amp;rft.edition=4th&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D2sIDAAAAQAAJ%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.place=Glasgow&amp;rft.pub=Hutchison+%26+Brookman&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li>"<a class="external text" href="http://docs.cltk.org/en/latest/latin.html" rel="nofollow">Classical Language Toolkit</a>" (CLTK). A <a href="/wiki/Natural_language_processing" title="Natural language processing">Natural Language Processing</a> toolkit for <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> offering a variety of functionality for Latin and other classical languages.</li>
</ul>
